        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: European influence (c. 1830 – 1956)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Francophonie" title="Francophonie">
        Francophonie
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Algeria,_Morocco_and_Tunis_(XIX_century).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3981" data-file-width="5478" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg/220px-Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg/330px-Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg/440px-Algeria%2C_Morocco_and_Tunis_%28XIX_century%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Algeria,_Morocco_and_Tunis_(XIX_century).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Maghreb in the second half of the 19th century
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2674" data-file-width="3497" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG/220px-Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG/330px-Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG/440px-Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morocco_Fez_Embroidery_Horse_Cover.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Moroccan
         <a href="/wiki/Fly_mask" title="Fly mask">
          fly mask
         </a>
         embroidery
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The successful
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       efforts to control the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
        Atlantic
       </a>
       coast in the 15th century did not affect the interior of Morocco. After the
       <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
        Napoleonic Wars
       </a>
       , North Africa became increasingly ungovernable from
       <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
        Istanbul
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       . As a result, it became the resort of pirates under local
       <a href="/wiki/Bey" title="Bey">
        beys
       </a>
       . The Maghreb also had far greater known wealth than the rest of Africa, and its location near the entrance to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea" title="Mediterranean sea">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       gave it strategic importance.
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       showed a strong interest in Morocco as early as 1830.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The Alaouite dynasty succeeded in maintaining the independence of Morocco in the 18th and 19th centuries, while other states in the region succumbed to
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman
       </a>
       , French, or
       <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
        British
       </a>
       domination.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In the latter part of the 19th century Morocco's instability resulted in European countries intervening to protect investments and to demand economic concessions. The first years of the 20th century saw major diplomatic efforts by European powers, especially France, to further its interests in the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1890s, the French administration and military in
       <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">
        Algiers
       </a>
       called for the annexation of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touat" title="Touat">
        Touat
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gourara" title="Gourara">
        Gourara
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tidikelt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tidikelt (page does not exist)">
        Tidikelt
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a complex that had been part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
        Moroccan
       </a>
       Empire for many centuries prior to the arrival of the French in Algeria.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An armed conflict opposed French 19th Corps Oran and Algiers divisions to the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=A%C3%AFt_Khabbash&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aït Khabbash (page does not exist)">
        Aït Khabbash
       </a>
       , a fraction of the Aït Ounbgui
       <i>
        khams
       </i>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFt_Atta" title="Aït Atta">
        Aït Atta
       </a>
       confederation. The conflict ended by the annexation of the Touat-Gourara-Tidikelt complex by France in 1901.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Recognition by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       of France's "
       <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">
        sphere of influence
       </a>
       " in Morocco in the 1904
       <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">
        Entente Cordiale
       </a>
       provoked a German reaction; the "crisis" of 1905–1906 was resolved at the
       <a href="/wiki/Algeciras_Conference" title="Algeciras Conference">
        Algeciras Conference
       </a>
       (1906), which formalized France's "special position" and entrusted policing of Morocco jointly to France and Spain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Prior to independence, Morocco was home to half a million
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">
        Europeans
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">
        European
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">
        Christians
       </a>
       formed almost half the population of the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Casablanca" title="Casablanca">
        Casablanca
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_history_of_the_Arab_peoples_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-A_history_of_the_Arab_peoples-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the kingdom's independence in 1956, the European population has decreased substantially.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="French_and_Spanish_protectorate_.281912_.E2.80.93_1956.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French_and_Spanish_protectorate_(1912_–_1956)">
        French and Spanish protectorate (1912 – 1956)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: French and Spanish protectorate (1912 – 1956)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/French_protectorate_in_Morocco" title="French protectorate in Morocco">
        French protectorate in Morocco
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_protectorate_in_Morocco" title="Spanish protectorate in Morocco">
        Spanish protectorate in Morocco
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maroc_preco.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1372" data-file-width="1885" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Maroc_preco.jpg/220px-Maroc_preco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Maroc_preco.jpg/330px-Maroc_preco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Maroc_preco.jpg/440px-Maroc_preco.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maroc_preco.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The French
         <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">
          artillery
         </a>
         at
         <a href="/wiki/Rabat" title="Rabat">
          Rabat
         </a>
         in 1911
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A second "Moroccan crisis" increased tensions among the powerful European countries, and resulted in the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fez" title="Treaty of Fez">
        Treaty of Fez
       </a>
       (signed on March 30, 1912), which made Morocco a
       <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">
        protectorate
       </a>
       of France. By a
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_Between_France_and_Spain_Regarding_Morocco" title="Treaty Between France and Spain Regarding Morocco">
        second treaty
       </a>
       signed by the French and Spanish heads of state,
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       was granted a Zone of influence in northern and southern Morocco on November 27, 1912. The northern part became the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_protectorate_in_Morocco" title="Spanish protectorate in Morocco">
        Spanish protectorate in Morocco
       </a>
       , while the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cabo_Juby" title="Cabo Juby">
        southern part
       </a>
       was ruled from El Aiun as a buffer zone between the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Sahara" title="Spanish Sahara">
        Spanish Colony of Rio de Oro
       </a>
       and Morocco. The treaty of Fez triggered the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1912_Fes_riots" title="1912 Fes riots">
        1912 Fez riots
       </a>
       . By the
       <a href="/wiki/Tangier_Protocol" title="Tangier Protocol">
        Tangier Protocol
       </a>
       signed in December 1923, Tangier received special status and became an
       <a href="/wiki/Tangier_International_Zone" title="Tangier International Zone">
        international zone
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirschberg1981_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hirschberg1981-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The treaties did not legally deprive Morocco of its status as a sovereign state, and the sultan remained the country's leader.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In practice, the sultan had no real power and the country was ruled by a colonial administration.
      </p>
      <p>
       Under the protectorate, French civil servants allied themselves with the French settlers and with their supporters in France to prevent any moves in the direction of Moroccan autonomy. As pacification proceeded, the French government focused on the exploitation of Morocco's mineral wealth, the creation of a modern transportation system, and the development of a modern agricultural sector geared to the French market. Tens of thousands of
       <i>
        colons
       </i>
       , or colonists, entered Morocco and acquired large tracts of the rich agricultural land.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_European_control">
        Opposition to European control
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Opposition to European control">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Morocco" title="French Morocco">
        French Morocco
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

         <img alt="File:1955-07-21 Morocco Riots Terror Mounts In Revolt Of Arabs.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/1955-07-21_Morocco_Riots_Terror_Mounts_In_Revolt_Of_Arabs.ogv/220px--1955-07-21_Morocco_Riots_Terror_Mounts_In_Revolt_Of_Arabs.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/1955-07-21_Morocco_Riots_Terror_Mounts_In_Revolt_Of_Arabs.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1955-07-21_Morocco_Riots_Terror_Mounts_In_Revolt_Of_Arabs.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Morocco riots overrun Casablanca due to discontent with French rule.
         <a href="/wiki/Universal_Newsreel" title="Universal Newsreel">
          Universal Newsreel
         </a>
         , 21 July 1955
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The separatist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Rif" title="Republic of the Rif">
        Republic of the Rif
       </a>
       was declared on 18 September 1921, by the people of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rif" title="Rif">
        Rif
       </a>
       . It was dissolved by Spanish and French forces on 27 May 1926.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 1934, a small group of nationalists, members of the newly formed
       <i>
        Comité d'Action Marocaine
       </i>
       , or
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moroccan_Action_Committee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Moroccan Action Committee (page does not exist)">
        Moroccan Action Committee
       </a>
       (CAM), proposed a Plan of Reforms that called for a return to indirect rule as envisaged by the Treaty of Fez, admission of Moroccans to government positions, and establishment of representative councils. CAM used petitions, newspaper editorials, and personal appeals to French officials to further its cause, but these proved inadequate, and the tensions created in the CAM by the failure of the plan caused it to split. The CAM was reconstituted as a nationalist political party to gain mass support for more radical demands, but the French suppressed the party in 1937.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Nationalist political parties, which subsequently arose under the French protectorate, based their arguments for Moroccan independence on declarations such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Charter" title="Atlantic Charter">
        Atlantic Charter
       </a>
       , a joint United States-British statement that set forth, among other things, the right of all peoples to choose the form of government under which they live.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The French regime also faced the opposition of the tribes — when the Berber were required to come under the jurisdiction of French courts in 1930, it increased support for the independence movement.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moroccan_Goumiere&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Moroccan Goumiere (page does not exist)">
        Moroccan
        <i>
         Goumiere
        </i>
       </a>
       , or indigenous soldiers in the French army, assisted the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allies" title="Allies">
        Allies
       </a>
       in both
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       During World War II, the badly divided nationalist movement became more cohesive. However, the nationalists belief that an Allied victory would pave the way for independence was disappointed.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In January 1944, the
       <a href="/wiki/Istiqlal_Party" title="Istiqlal Party">
        Istiqlal (Independence) Party
       </a>
       , which subsequently provided most of the leadership for the nationalist movement, released a manifesto demanding full independence, national reunification, and a democratic constitution. The Sultan
       <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_V_of_Morocco" title="Mohammed V of Morocco">
        Muhammad V
       </a>
       (1927–1961) had approved the manifesto before its submission to the French resident general, who answered that no basic change in the protectorate status was being considered.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The general sympathy of the sultan for the nationalists became evident by the end of the war, although he still hoped to see complete independence achieved gradually. By contrast, the residency, supported by French economic interests and vigorously backed by most of the
       <i>
        colons
       </i>
       , adamantly refused to consider even reforms short of independence.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 1952, a riot broke out in Casablanca over the murder of a Tunisian labor leader; this event marked a watershed in relations between Moroccan political parties and French authorities. In the aftermath of the rioting, the residency outlawed the new Moroccan Communist Party and the
       <a href="/wiki/Istiqlal_Party" title="Istiqlal Party">
        Istiqlal
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       France's
       <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">
        exile
       </a>
       of the highly respected
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultan_Mohammed_V" title="Sultan Mohammed V">
        Sultan Mohammed V
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">
        Madagascar
       </a>
       in 1953 and his replacement by the unpopular
       <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Ben_Aarafa" title="Mohammed Ben Aarafa">
        Mohammed Ben Aarafa
       </a>
       , sparked active opposition to the French protectorate both from nationalists and those who saw the sultan as a religious leader. Two years later, faced with a united Moroccan demand for the sultan's return and rising violence in Morocco, as well as a deteriorating situation in Algeria, the French government brought Mohammed V back to Morocco, and the following year began the negotiations that led to Moroccan independence.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Independent_Morocco_.28since_1956.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Independent_Morocco_(since_1956)">
        Independent Morocco (since 1956)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Independent Morocco (since 1956)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_V_1954,_Madagascar.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="305" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Mohammed_V_1954%2C_Madagascar.jpg/180px-Mohammed_V_1954%2C_Madagascar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Mohammed_V_1954%2C_Madagascar.jpg/270px-Mohammed_V_1954%2C_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Mohammed_V_1954%2C_Madagascar.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_V_1954,_Madagascar.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_V_of_Morocco" title="Mohammed V of Morocco">
          Mohammed V
         </a>
         and his family in exile in Madagascar, 1954.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In late 1955, in the middle of what came to be known as the
       <b>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Revolution_of_the_King_and_the_People&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Revolution of the King and the People (page does not exist)">
         Revolution of the King and the People
        </a>
        <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
         (
         <a class="extiw" href="https://ar.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D8%AB%D9%88%D8%B1%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D9%84%D9%83_%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B4%D8%B9%D8%A8" title="ar:ثورة الملك والشعب">
          ar
         </a>
         )
        </span>
       </b>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sultan Mohammed V successfully negotiated the gradual restoration of Moroccan independence within a framework of French-Moroccan interdependence. The sultan agreed to institute reforms that would transform Morocco into a constitutional monarchy with a democratic form of government. In February 1956, Morocco acquired limited home rule. Further negotiations for full independence culminated in the French-Moroccan Agreement signed in Paris on March 2, 1956.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On April 7, 1956, France officially relinquished its protectorate in Morocco. The internationalized city of
       <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">
        Tangier
       </a>
       was reintegrated with the signing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Tangier_Protocol" title="Tangier Protocol">
        Tangier Protocol
       </a>
       on October 29, 1956. The abolition of the Spanish protectorate and the recognition of Moroccan independence by Spain were negotiated separately and made final in the Joint Declaration of April 1956.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Through this agreement with Spain in 1956 and another in 1958, Moroccan control over certain Spanish-ruled areas was restored. Attempts
       <a href="/wiki/Ifni_War" title="Ifni War">
        to claim other Spanish possessions through military action
       </a>
       were less successful.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the months that followed independence, Mohammed V proceeded to build a modern governmental structure under a
       <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">
        constitutional monarchy
       </a>
       in which the sultan would exercise an active political role. He acted cautiously, intent on preventing the Istiqlal from consolidating its control and establishing a
       <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">
        one-party state
       </a>
       . He assumed the monarchy in 1957.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Reign_of_Hassan_II_.281961_.E2.80.93_1999.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Hassan_II_(1961_–_1999)">
        Reign of Hassan II (1961 – 1999)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Reign of Hassan II (1961 – 1999)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hassan_II,_Marrakech,_1966.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1618" data-file-width="2160" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg/220px-Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg/330px-Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg/440px-Hassan_II%2C_Marrakech%2C_1966.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hassan_II,_Marrakech,_1966.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         King
         <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_of_Morocco" title="Hassan II of Morocco">
          Hassan II
         </a>
         , on his way to Friday prayers in
         <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">
          Marrakesh
         </a>
         , 1967.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Mohammed V's son
       <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_of_Morocco" title="Hassan II of Morocco">
        Hassan II
       </a>
       became King of Morocco on March 3, 1961. His rule saw significant political unrest, and the ruthless government response earned the period the name "the
       <a href="/wiki/Years_of_Lead_(Morocco)" title="Years of Lead (Morocco)">
        years of lead
       </a>
       ". Hassan took personal control of the government as prime minister, and named a new cabinet. Aided by an advisory council, he drew up a new constitution, which was approved overwhelmingly in a December 1962 referendum. Under its provisions, the king remained the central figure in the executive branch of the government, but legislative power was vested in a bicameral parliament, and an independent judiciary was guaranteed.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In May 1963, legislative elections took place for the first time, and the royalist coalition secured a small plurality of seats. However, following a period of political upheaval in June 1965, Hassan II assumed full legislative and executive powers under a "state of exception," which remained in effect until 1970.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Subsequently, a reform constitution was approved, restoring limited parliamentary government, and new elections were held. However, dissent remained, revolving around complaints of widespread corruption and malfeasance in government. In July 1971 and again in August 1972, the regime was challenged by two
       <a href="/wiki/1972_Moroccan_coup_attempt" title="1972 Moroccan coup attempt">
        attempted military coups
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After neighbouring
       <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">
        Algeria
       </a>
       's 1962 independence from
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       , border skirmishes in the
       <a href="/wiki/Tindouf_Province" title="Tindouf Province">
        Tindouf area
       </a>
       of south-western Algeria escalated in 1963 into what is known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Sand_War" title="Sand War">
        Sand War
       </a>
       . The conflict ended after
       <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">
        Organisation of African Unity
       </a>
       mediation, with no territorial changes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The patriotism engendered by Morocco’s participation in the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       conflict and by the events in Western Sahara contributed to Hassan’s popularity. The king had dispatched Moroccan troops to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai" title="Sinai">
        Sinai
       </a>
       front after the outbreak of the
       <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">
        Arab-Israeli War
       </a>
       in October 1973. Although they arrived too late to engage in hostilities, the action won Morocco goodwill among other Arab states.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Soon after, the attention of the government turned to the acquisition of
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">
        Western Sahara
       </a>
       from Spain, an issue on which all major domestic parties agreed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Western_Sahara_conflict_.281974_.E2.80.93_1991.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Sahara_conflict_(1974_–_1991)">
        Western Sahara conflict (1974 – 1991)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Western Sahara conflict (1974 – 1991)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_Sahara" title="History of Western Sahara">
        History of Western Sahara
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Spanish
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclave" title="Enclave">
        enclave
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Ifni" title="Ifni">
        Ifni
       </a>
       in the south became part of the new state of Morocco in 1969, but other Spanish possessions in the north, including
       <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">
        Ceuta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Melilla" title="Melilla">
        Melilla
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plaza_de_soberan%C3%ADa" title="Plaza de soberanía">
        Plaza de soberanía
       </a>
       , remained under Spanish control, with Morocco viewing them as
       <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">
        occupied
       </a>
       territory.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In August 1974, Spain formally acknowledged the 1966
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
        United Nations
       </a>
       (UN) resolution calling for a referendum on the future status of the Western Sahara, and requested that a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebiscite" title="Plebiscite">
        plebiscite
       </a>
       be conducted under UN supervision. A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_visiting_mission_to_Spanish_Sahara" title="United Nations visiting mission to Spanish Sahara">
        UN visiting mission
       </a>
       reported in October 1975 that an overwhelming majority of the Saharan people desired independence. Morocco protested the proposed referendum and took its case to the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">
        International Court of Justice
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">
        The Hague
       </a>
       , which ruled that despite historical “ties of allegiance” between Morocco and the tribes of Western Sahara, there was no legal justification for departing from the UN position on self-determination. Spain, meanwhile, had declared that even in the absence of a referendum, it intended to surrender political control of Western Sahara, and Spain, Morocco, and
       <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">
        Mauritania
       </a>
       convened a
       <a href="/wiki/Madrid_Accords" title="Madrid Accords">
        tripartite conference
       </a>
       to resolve the territory’s future. Spain also announced that it was opening independence talks with the Algerian-backed Saharan independence movement known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Polisario_Front" title="Polisario Front">
        Polisario Front
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In early 1976, Spain ceded the administration of the Western Sahara to Morocco and Mauritania. Morocco assumed control over the northern two-thirds of the territory, and conceded the remaining portion in the south to Mauritania. An assembly of Saharan tribal leaders duly acknowledged Moroccan sovereignty. However, buoyed by the increasing defection of tribal chiefs to its cause, the Polisario drew up a constitution, and announced the formation of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saharawi_Arab_Democratic_Republic" title="Saharawi Arab Democratic Republic">
        Saharawi Arab Democratic Republic
       </a>
       (SADR), and itself formed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government-in-exile" title="Government-in-exile">
        government-in-exile
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Moroccan government eventually sent a large portion of its combat forces into Western Sahara to confront the Polisario’s forces, which were relatively small but well-equipped, highly mobile, and resourceful. The Polisario used Algerian bases for quick strikes against targets deep inside Morocco and Mauritania, as well as for operations in Western Sahara. In August 1979, after suffering military losses, Mauritania renounced its claim to Western Sahara and signed a peace treaty with the Polisario. Morocco then annexed the entire territory and, in 1985 built a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moroccan_Wall" title="Moroccan Wall">
        2,500-kilometer sand berm
       </a>
       around three-quarters of Western Sahara.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1988, Morocco and the Polisario Front agreed on a United Nations (UN) peace plan, and a cease-fire and settlement plan went into effect in 1991. Even though the UN Security Council created a
       <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">
        peacekeeping
       </a>
       force to implement a referendum on self-determination for Western Sahara, it has yet to be held, periodic negotiations have failed, and the status of the territory remains unresolved.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The war against the Polisario guerrillas put severe strains on the economy, and Morocco found itself increasingly isolated diplomatically. Gradual political reforms in the 1990s culminated in the constitutional reform of 1996, which created a new bicameral legislature with expanded, although still limited, powers. Elections for the Chamber of Representatives were held in 1997, reportedly marred by irregularities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Reign_of_Mohammed_VI_.28since_1999.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Mohammed_VI_(since_1999)">
        Reign of Mohammed VI (since 1999)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Morocco&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reign of Mohammed VI (since 1999)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       With the death of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Morocco" title="King of Morocco">
        King
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_of_Morocco" title="Hassan II of Morocco">
        Hassan II of Morocco
       </a>
       in 1999, the more
       <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">
        liberal
       </a>
       Crown Prince Sidi Mohammed took the throne, assuming the title Mohammed VI. He enacted successive reforms to modernize Morocco, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human-rights" title="Human-rights">
        human-rights
       </a>
       record of the country improved markedly.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       One of the new king’s first acts was to free approximately 8,000 political prisoners and reduce the sentences of another 30,000. He also established a commission to compensate families of missing political activists and others subjected to arbitrary detention.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In September 2002, new legislative elections were held, and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Socialiste_des_Forces_Populaires" title="Union Socialiste des Forces Populaires">
        Socialist Union of Popular Forces
       </a>
       (USFP) won a plurality. International observers regarded the elections as free and fair, noting the absence of the irregularities that had plagued the 1997 elections. In May 2003, in honor of the birth of a son, the king ordered the release of 9,000 prisoners and the reduction of 38,000 sentences. Also in 2003,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber-language" title="Berber-language">
        Berber-language
       </a>
       instruction was introduced in primary schools, prior to introducing it at all educational levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2000, women's groups organized demonstrations in
       <a href="/wiki/Rabat" title="Rabat">
        Rabat
       </a>
       proposing reforms to the legal status of women in the country. 200,000 to 300,000 women attended, calling for
       <a href="/wiki/Polygamy_in_Morocco" title="Polygamy in Morocco">
        a ban on polygamy
       </a>
       , and the introduction of civil
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divorce_law" title="Divorce law">
        divorce law
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although a counter-demonstration attracted 200,000 to 400,000 participants, the movement was influential on King Mohammed, and he enacted a new
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mudawana" title="Mudawana">
         Mudawana
        </a>
       </i>
       , or family law, in early 2004, meeting some of the demands of women's rights activists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In July 2002, a
       <a href="/wiki/Perejil_Island_crisis" title="Perejil Island crisis">
        crisis broke out
       </a>
       with Spain over a
       <a href="/wiki/Perejil_Island" title="Perejil Island">
        small, uninhabited island
       </a>
       lying just less than 200 meters from the Moroccan coast, named Toura or Leila by Moroccans and Perejil by Spain. After mediation by the United States, both Morocco and Spain agreed to return to the status quo, under which the island remains deserted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Internationally, Morocco has maintained strong ties to the West. It was one of the first Arab and Islamic states to denounce the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9/11_terrorist_attacks" title="9/11 terrorist attacks">
        9/11 terrorist attacks
       </a>
       on the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In May 2003, Islamist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicide_bomber" title="Suicide bomber">
        suicide bombers
       </a>
       simultaneously
       <a href="/wiki/2003_Casablanca_bombings" title="2003 Casablanca bombings">
        struck a series of sites in Casablanca
       </a>
       , killing 45 and injuring more than 100 others. The Moroccan government responded with a crackdown against Islamist extremists, ultimately arresting several thousand, prosecuting 1,200, and sentencing about 900. Additional arrests followed in June 2004. That same month, the United States designated Morocco a major non-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">
        North Atlantic Treaty Organization
       </a>
       ally, stating that it was in recognition of its efforts to thwart international terrorism. On January 1, 2006, a comprehensive bilateral
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_trade_agreement" title="Free trade agreement">
        free trade agreement
       </a>
       between the United States and Morocco took effect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOCprof_55-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOCprof-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The agreement had been signed in 2004 along with a similar agreement with the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       , Morocco's main trade partner.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>

          <span class="reference-text">
           Frank E. Trout,
           <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/216479" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Morocco's Boundary in the Guir-Zousfana River Basin
            </i>
           </a>
           , in: African Historical Studies, Vol. 3, No. 1 (1970), pp. 37-56, Publ. Boston University African Studies Center: « The Algerian-Moroccan conflict can be said to have begun in the 1890s when the administration and military in Algeria called for annexation of the Touat-Gourara-Tidikelt, a sizable expanse of Saharan oases that was nominally a part of the Moroccan Empire (...) The Touat-Gourara-Tidikelt oases had been an appendage of the Moroccan Empire, jutting southeast for about 750 kilometers into the Saharan desert »
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-48">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-48">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Frank E. Trout,
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=IO69HppDTDgC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;hl=fr" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Morocco's Saharan Frontiers
            </i>
           </a>
           , Droz (1969), p.24 (
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9782600044950" title="Special:BookSources/9782600044950">
            9782600044950
           </a>
           ) : « The Gourara-Touat-Tidikelt complex had been under Moroccan domination for many centuries prior to the arrival of the French in Algeria »
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-49">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-49">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Claude Lefébure,
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.persee.fr/web/revues/home/prescript/article/remmm_0035-1474_1986_num_41_1_2114" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Ayt Khebbach, impasse sud-est. L'involution d'une tribu marocaine exclue du Sahara
            </i>
           </a>
           , in: Revue de l'Occident musulman et de la Méditerranée, N°41-42, 1986. Désert et montagne au Maghreb. pp. 136-157: « les Divisions d'Oran et d'Alger du 19e Corps d'armée n'ont pu conquérir le Touat et le Gourara qu'au prix de durs combats menés contre les semi-nomades d'obédience marocaine qui, depuis plus d'un siècle, imposaient leur protection aux oasiens »
          </span>
         </li>
